<p>With low to medium confidence, Schneider <i>et al.</i> (2007)<sup id="cite_ref-table_of_key_vulnerabilities_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-table_of_key_vulnerabilities-20">[20]</a></sup> projected that for about a 1 to 3&#160;°C increase in global mean temperature (by the years 2090-2100, relative to average temperatures in the years 1990–2000), there would be productivity decreases for some <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">cereals</a> in low latitudes, and productivity increases in high latitudes. With medium confidence, global production potential was projected to:<sup id="cite_ref-table_of_key_vulnerabilities_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-table_of_key_vulnerabilities-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>increase up to around 3&#160;°C,</li>
<li>very likely decrease above about 3&#160;°C.</li>
</ul>
<p>Most of the studies on global agriculture assessed by Schneider <i>et al.</i> (2007)<sup id="cite_ref-schneider_climate_change_and_agriculture_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schneider_climate_change_and_agriculture-107">[107]</a></sup> had not incorporated a number of critical factors, including changes in extreme events, or the spread of pests and diseases. Studies had also not considered the development of specific practices or technologies to aid <a href="/wiki/Adaptation_to_global_warming#Agricultural_production" class="mw-redirect" title="Adaptation to global warming">adaptation to climate change</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-schneider_climate_change_and_agriculture_107-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schneider_climate_change_and_agriculture-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>The graphs opposite show the projected effects of climate change on selected crop yields.<sup id="cite_ref-us_nrc_2011_text_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-us_nrc_2011_text-111">[111]</a></sup> Actual changes in yields may be above or below these central estimates.<sup id="cite_ref-us_nrc_2011_text_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-us_nrc_2011_text-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>The projections above can be expressed relative to pre-industrial (1750) temperatures.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> 0.6&#160;°C of warming is estimated to have occurred between 1750 and 1990-2000. Add 0.6&#160;°C to the above projections to convert them from a 1990-2000 to pre-industrial baseline.</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_security">Food security</span></h5>
<p>Easterling <i>et al.</i> (2007)<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> assessed studies that made quantitative projections of climate change impacts on <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">food security</a>. It was noted that these projections were highly uncertain and had limitations. However, the assessed studies suggested a number of fairly robust findings. The first was that climate change would likely increase the number of people at risk of <a href="/wiki/Hunger" title="Hunger">hunger</a> compared with reference scenarios with no climate change. Climate change impacts depended strongly on projected future social and economic development. Additionally, the magnitude of climate change impacts was projected to be smaller compared to the impact of social and economic development. In 2006, the global estimate for the number of people <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">undernourished</a> was 820 million.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Under the SRES A1, B1, and B2 scenarios (see the <a href="/wiki/Special_Report_on_Emissions_Scenarios#Scenario_families" title="Special Report on Emissions Scenarios">SRES article</a> for information on each scenario group), projections for the year 2080 showed a reduction in the number of people undernourished of about 560-700&#160;million people, with a global total of undernourished people of 100-240&#160;million in 2080. By contrast, the SRES A2 scenario showed only a small decrease in the risk of hunger from 2006 levels. The smaller reduction under A2 was attributed to the higher projected future population level in this scenario.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Droughts_and_agriculture">Droughts and agriculture</span></h4>
<p>Some evidence suggests that droughts have been occurring more frequently because of global warming and they are expected to become more frequent and intense in Africa, southern Europe, the Middle East, most of the Americas, Australia, and Southeast Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> However, other research suggests that there has been little change in drought over the past 60 years.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Their impacts are aggravated because of increased water demand, population growth, urban expansion, and environmental protection efforts in many areas.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Droughts result in crop failures and the loss of pasture grazing land for livestock.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_global_warming_on_human_health" title="Effects of global warming on human health">Effects of global warming on human health</a></div>
<p>Human beings are exposed to climate change through changing weather patterns (temperature, precipitation, sea-level rise and more frequent extreme events) and indirectly through changes in water, air and food quality and changes in ecosystems, agriculture, industry and settlements and the economy (Confalonieri <i>et al.</i>, 2007:393).<sup id="cite_ref-health_summary_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-health_summary-59">[59]</a></sup> According to an assessment of the scientific literature by Confalonieri <i>et al.</i> (2007:393),<sup id="cite_ref-health_summary_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-health_summary-59">[59]</a></sup> the effects of climate change to date have been small, but are projected to progressively increase in all countries and regions.</p>
<p>A study by the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO, 2009)<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> estimated the effect of climate change on human health. Not all of the effects of climate change were included in their estimates, for example, the effects of more frequent and extreme storms were excluded. Climate change was estimated to have been responsible for 3% of <a href="/wiki/Diarrhoea" class="mw-redirect" title="Diarrhoea">diarrhoea</a>, 3% of <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>, and 3.8% of <a href="/wiki/Dengue_fever" title="Dengue fever">dengue fever</a> deaths worldwide in 2004. Total attributable mortality was about 0.2% of deaths in 2004; of these, 85% were child deaths.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Projections_2">Projections</span></h4>
<p>With high confidence, authors of the IPCC AR4 Synthesis report<sup id="cite_ref-IPCC_AR4_Synthesis_Report_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_AR4_Synthesis_Report-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:48</sup> projected that climate change would bring some benefits in temperate areas, such as fewer deaths from cold exposure, and some mixed effects such as changes in range and transmission potential of <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a> in <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>. Benefits were projected to be outweighed by negative health effects of rising temperatures, especially in developing countries.</p>
<p>With very high confidence, Confalonieri <i>et al.</i> (2007)<sup id="cite_ref-health_summary_59-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-health_summary-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:393</sup> concluded that economic development was an important component of possible adaptation to climate change. Economic growth on its own, however, was not judged to be sufficient to insulate the world's population from disease and injury due to climate change. Future vulnerability to climate change will depend not only on the extent of social and economic change, but also on how the benefits and costs of change are distributed in society.<sup id="cite_ref-Future_vulnerability_to_climate_change_health_impacts_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Future_vulnerability_to_climate_change_health_impacts-121">[121]</a></sup> For example, in the 19th century, rapid <a href="/wiki/Urbanization" title="Urbanization">urbanization</a> in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">western Europe</a> lead to a plummeting in population health.<sup id="cite_ref-Future_vulnerability_to_climate_change_health_impacts_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Future_vulnerability_to_climate_change_health_impacts-121">[121]</a></sup> Other factors important in determining the health of populations include <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">education</a>, the availability of health services, and <a href="/wiki/Public_health" title="Public health">public-health</a> <a href="/wiki/Infrastructure" title="Infrastructure">infrastructure</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-health_summary_59-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-health_summary-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:393</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_resources">Water resources</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Water_security" title="Water security">Water security</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NOAAprecipitationtrend.jpg" class="image"><img alt="refer to caption" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/NOAAprecipitationtrend.jpg/220px-NOAAprecipitationtrend.jpg" width="220" height="325" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/NOAAprecipitationtrend.jpg/330px-NOAAprecipitationtrend.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/NOAAprecipitationtrend.jpg/440px-NOAAprecipitationtrend.jpg 2x" data-file-width="649" data-file-height="959" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NOAAprecipitationtrend.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Precipitation during the 20th century and up through 2008 during global warming, the <a href="/wiki/NOAA" class="mw-redirect" title="NOAA">NOAA</a> estimating an observed trend over that period of 1.87% global precipitation increase per century.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A number of climate-related trends have been observed that affect <a href="/wiki/Water_resources" title="Water resources">water resources</a>. These include changes in precipitation, the crysosphere and <a href="/wiki/Surface_water" title="Surface water">surface waters</a> (e.g., changes in <a href="/wiki/River_flows" class="mw-redirect" title="River flows">river flows</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Observed and projected impacts of climate change on <a href="/wiki/Freshwater" class="mw-redirect" title="Freshwater">freshwater</a> systems and their management are mainly due to changes in temperature, sea level and precipitation variability.<sup id="cite_ref-freshwater_impacts_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-freshwater_impacts-123">[123]</a></sup> Sea level rise will extend areas of <a href="/wiki/Salinization" class="mw-redirect" title="Salinization">salinization</a> of <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a> and <a href="/wiki/Estuary" title="Estuary">estuaries</a>, resulting in a decrease in freshwater availability for humans and ecosystems in coastal areas. In an assessment of the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_literature" title="Scientific literature">scientific literature</a>, Kundzewicz <i>et al.</i> (2007)<sup id="cite_ref-freshwater_impacts_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-freshwater_impacts-123">[123]</a></sup> concluded, with high confidence, that:</p>
<ul>
<li>the negative impacts of climate change on freshwater systems outweigh the benefits. All of the regions assessed in the <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Fourth_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Fourth Assessment Report">IPCC Fourth Assessment Report</a> (<a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polar_region" class="mw-redirect" title="Polar region">Polar regions</a> (<a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antarctic" title="Antarctic">Antarctic</a>), and small islands) showed an overall net negative impact of climate change on water resources and freshwater ecosystems.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Semi-arid_climate" title="Semi-arid climate">Semi-arid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arid" title="Arid">arid</a> areas are particularly exposed to the impacts of climate change on freshwater. With very high confidence, it was judged that many of these areas, e.g., the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_basin" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean basin">Mediterranean basin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_United_States" title="Western United States">Western United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">Southern Africa</a>, and north-eastern <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, would suffer a decrease in water resources due to climate change.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Migration_and_conflict">Migration and conflict</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_migrant" title="Environmental migrant">Environmental migrant</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Global_climate_model" class="mw-redirect" title="Global climate model">General circulation models</a> project that the future climate change will bring wetter coasts, drier mid-continent areas, and further sea level rise.<sup id="cite_ref-sar_migration_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sar_migration-124">[124]</a></sup> Such changes could result in the gravest effects of climate change through <a href="/wiki/Human_migration" title="Human migration">human migration</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> Millions might be displaced by <a href="/wiki/Shoreline_erosion" class="mw-redirect" title="Shoreline erosion">shoreline erosions</a>, river and coastal <a href="/wiki/Flood" title="Flood">flooding</a>, or severe <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">drought</a>.</p>
<p>Migration related to climate change is likely to be predominantly from <a href="/wiki/Rural_area" title="Rural area">rural areas</a> in <a href="/wiki/Developing_countries" class="mw-redirect" title="Developing countries">developing countries</a> to towns and <a href="/wiki/Cities" class="mw-redirect" title="Cities">cities</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sar_migration_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sar_migration-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:407</sup><sup id="cite_ref-wdr_migration_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wdr_migration-126">[126]</a></sup> In the short term climate stress is likely to add incrementally to existing migration patterns rather than generating entirely new flows of people.<sup id="cite_ref-wdr_migration_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wdr_migration-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:110</sup></p>
<p>It has been argued that environmental degradation, loss of access to resources (e.g., water resources),<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> and resulting human migration could become a source of political and even <a href="/wiki/Military_conflict" class="mw-redirect" title="Military conflict">military conflict</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-tar_migration_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tar_migration-128">[128]</a></sup> Factors other than climate change may, however, be more important in affecting conflict. For example, Wilbanks <i>et al.</i> (2007)<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> suggested that major environmentally influenced conflicts in Africa were more to do with the relative abundance of resources, e.g., <a href="/wiki/Oil" title="Oil">oil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamonds</a>, than with resource scarcity. Scott <i>et al.</i> (2001) placed only low confidence in predictions of increased conflict due to climate change.<sup id="cite_ref-tar_migration_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tar_migration-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2013 study found that significant climatic changes were associated with a higher risk of conflict worldwide, and predicted that "amplified rates of human conflict could represent a large and critical social impact of anthropogenic climate change in both low- and high-income countries."<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Similarly, a 2014 study found that higher temperatures were associated with a greater likelihood of violent crime, and predicted that global warming would cause millions of such crimes in the United States alone during the 21st century.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>Military planners are concerned that global warming is a "threat multiplier". "Whether it is poverty, food and water scarcity, diseases, economic instability, or threat of natural disasters, the broad range of changing climatic conditions may be far reaching. These challenges may threaten stability in much of the world".<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aggregate_impacts">Aggregate impacts</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economic_impacts_of_climate_change" title="Economic impacts of climate change">Economic impacts of climate change</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Aggregate_data" title="Aggregate data">Aggregating</a> impacts adds up the total impact of climate change across sectors and/or regions.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> Examples of aggregate measures include <a href="/wiki/Economic_impacts_of_climate_change#Distribution_of_impacts" title="Economic impacts of climate change">economic cost</a> (e.g., changes in <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a> (GDP) and the <a href="/wiki/Economic_impacts_of_climate_change#Marginal_impacts" title="Economic impacts of climate change">social cost of carbon</a>), changes in ecosystems (e.g., changes over land area from one type of vegetation to another),<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> human health impacts, and the number of people affected by climate change.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> Aggregate measures such as economic cost require researchers to make <a href="/wiki/Value_judgement" class="mw-redirect" title="Value judgement">value judgements</a> over the importance of impacts occurring in different regions and at different times.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Observed_impacts_2">Observed impacts</span></h4>
<p>Global losses reveal rapidly rising costs due to extreme weather-related events since the 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Socioeconomics" title="Socioeconomics">Socio-economic</a> factors have contributed to the observed trend of global losses, e.g., population growth, increased <a href="/wiki/Wealth" title="Wealth">wealth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-disaster_losses_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-disaster_losses-137">[137]</a></sup> Part of the growth is also related to regional climatic factors, e.g., changes in precipitation and flooding events. It is difficult to quantify the relative impact of socio-economic factors and climate change on the observed trend.<sup id="cite_ref-disaster_losses_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-disaster_losses-137">[137]</a></sup> The trend does, however, suggest increasing vulnerability of social systems to climate change.<sup id="cite_ref-disaster_losses_137-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-disaster_losses-137">[137]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Projected_impacts_2">Projected impacts</span></h4>
<p>The total economic impacts from climate change are highly uncertain.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> With medium confidence, Smith <i>et al.</i> (2001)<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_estimate_of_climate_change_impacts_on_GDP_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_estimate_of_climate_change_impacts_on_GDP-140">[140]</a></sup> concluded that world GDP would change by plus or minus a few percent for a small increase in global mean temperature (up to around 2&#160;°C relative to the 1990 temperature level). Most studies assessed by Smith <i>et al.</i> (2001)<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_estimate_of_climate_change_impacts_on_GDP_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_estimate_of_climate_change_impacts_on_GDP-140">[140]</a></sup> projected losses in world GDP for a medium increase in global mean temperature (above 2-3&#160;°C relative to the 1990 temperature level), with increasing losses for greater temperature increases. This assessment is consistent with the findings of more recent studies, as reviewed by Hitz and Smith (2004).<sup id="cite_ref-update_of_reasons_for_concern_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-update_of_reasons_for_concern-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>Economic impacts are expected to vary regionally.<sup id="cite_ref-update_of_reasons_for_concern_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-update_of_reasons_for_concern-141">[141]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> For a medium increase in global mean temperature (2-3&#160;°C of warming, relative to the average temperature between 1990–2000), market sectors in low-latitude and less-developed areas might experience net costs due to climate change.<sup id="cite_ref-table_of_key_vulnerabilities_20-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-table_of_key_vulnerabilities-20">[20]</a></sup> On the other hand, market sectors in high-latitude and developed regions might experience net benefits for this level of warming. A global mean temperature increase above about 2-3&#160;°C (relative to 1990-2000) would very likely result in market sectors across all regions experiencing either declines in net benefits or rises in net costs.<sup id="cite_ref-magnitudes_of_impact_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-magnitudes_of_impact-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>Aggregate impacts have also been quantified in non-economic terms. For example, climate change over the 21st century is likely to adversely affect hundreds of millions of people through increased coastal flooding, reductions in water supplies, increased malnutrition and increased health impacts.<sup id="cite_ref-ar4_synthesis_reasons_for_concern_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ar4_synthesis_reasons_for_concern-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_systems">Biological systems</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_and_ecosystems" title="Climate change and ecosystems">Climate change and ecosystems</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Extinction_risk_from_global_warming" title="Extinction risk from global warming">Extinction risk from global warming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Effect_of_climate_change_on_plant_biodiversity" class="mw-redirect" title="Effect of climate change on plant biodiversity">Effect of climate change on plant biodiversity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_terrestrial_animals" title="Effects of climate change on terrestrial animals">Effects of climate change on terrestrial animals</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_marine_mammals" class="mw-redirect" title="Effects of climate change on marine mammals">Effects of climate change on marine mammals</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Observed_impacts_on_biological_systems">Observed impacts on biological systems</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A_vast_array_of_physical_and_biological_systems_across_the_Earth_are_being_affected_by_human-induced_global_warming_(low-res).png" class="image"><img alt="refer to caption" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/A_vast_array_of_physical_and_biological_systems_across_the_Earth_are_being_affected_by_human-induced_global_warming_%28low-res%29.png/350px-A_vast_array_of_physical_and_biological_systems_across_the_Earth_are_being_affected_by_human-induced_global_warming_%28low-res%29.png" width="350" height="460" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/A_vast_array_of_physical_and_biological_systems_across_the_Earth_are_being_affected_by_human-induced_global_warming_%28low-res%29.png/525px-A_vast_array_of_physical_and_biological_systems_across_the_Earth_are_being_affected_by_human-induced_global_warming_%28low-res%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/A_vast_array_of_physical_and_biological_systems_across_the_Earth_are_being_affected_by_human-induced_global_warming_%28low-res%29.png/700px-A_vast_array_of_physical_and_biological_systems_across_the_Earth_are_being_affected_by_human-induced_global_warming_%28low-res%29.png 2x" data-file-width="1725" data-file-height="2267" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A_vast_array_of_physical_and_biological_systems_across_the_Earth_are_being_affected_by_human-induced_global_warming_(low-res).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A vast array of physical and biological systems across the Earth are being affected by human-induced global warming.<sup id="cite_ref-nasa_briefs_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nasa_briefs-95">[95]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>With very high confidence, Rosenzweig <i>et al.</i> (2007) concluded that recent warming had strongly affected natural biological systems.<sup id="cite_ref-rosenzweig_observed_impacts_43-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rosenzweig_observed_impacts-43">[43]</a></sup> Hundreds of studies have documented responses of <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a> to the climate changes that have already occurred.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> For example, in the Northern Hemisphere, species are almost uniformly moving their ranges northward and up in elevation in search of cooler temperatures.<sup id="cite_ref-nrc_2008b_observed_ecosystem_impacts_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nrc_2008b_observed_ecosystem_impacts-144">[144]</a></sup> Humans are very likely causing changes in regional temperatures to which plants and animals are responding.<sup id="cite_ref-nrc_2008b_observed_ecosystem_impacts_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nrc_2008b_observed_ecosystem_impacts-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Projected_impacts_on_biological_systems">Projected impacts on biological systems</span></h3>
<p>By the year 2100, ecosystems will be exposed to atmospheric <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> levels substantially higher than in the past 650,000 years, and global temperatures at least among the highest of those experienced in the past 740,000 years.<sup id="cite_ref-fischlin_executive_summary_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fischlin_executive_summary-145">[145]</a></sup> Significant disruptions of ecosystems are projected to increase with future climate change.<sup id="cite_ref-tar_synthesis_ecosystems_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tar_synthesis_ecosystems-146">[146]</a></sup> Examples of disruptions include disturbances such as fire, drought, pest <a href="/wiki/Infestation" title="Infestation">infestation</a>, invasion of species, storms, and <a href="/wiki/Coral_bleaching" title="Coral bleaching">coral bleaching</a> events. The stresses caused by climate change, added to other stresses on ecological systems (e.g., land conversion, <a href="/wiki/Land_degradation" title="Land degradation">land degradation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harvest" title="Harvest">harvesting</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a>), threaten substantial damage to or complete loss of some unique ecosystems, and <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinction</a> of some critically endangered species.<sup id="cite_ref-tar_synthesis_ecosystems_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tar_synthesis_ecosystems-146">[146]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<p>Climate change has been estimated to be a major driver of <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a> loss in cool <a href="/wiki/Conifer" class="mw-redirect" title="Conifer">conifer</a> forests, <a href="/wiki/Savanna" title="Savanna">savannas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_climate" title="Mediterranean climate">mediterranean-climate</a> systems, <a href="/wiki/Tropical_forest" class="mw-redirect" title="Tropical forest">tropical forests</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_tundra" class="mw-redirect" title="Arctic tundra">Arctic tundra</a>, and in <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">coral reefs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-global_ecosystem_impacts_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-global_ecosystem_impacts-148">[148]</a></sup> In other ecosystems, <a href="/wiki/Land_use" title="Land use">land-use</a> change may be a stronger driver of biodiversity loss at least in the near-term.<sup id="cite_ref-global_ecosystem_impacts_148-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-global_ecosystem_impacts-148">[148]</a></sup> Beyond the year 2050, climate change may be the major driver for biodiversity loss globally.<sup id="cite_ref-global_ecosystem_impacts_148-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-global_ecosystem_impacts-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<p>A literature assessment by Fischlin <i>et al.</i> (2007)<sup id="cite_ref-fischlin_executive_summary_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fischlin_executive_summary-145">[145]</a></sup> included a quantitative estimate of the number of species at increased risk of extinction due to climate change. With medium confidence, it was projected that approximately 20 to 30% of plant and animal species assessed so far (in an unbiased <a href="/wiki/Sampling_(statistics)" title="Sampling (statistics)">sample</a>) would likely be at increasingly high risk of extinction should global mean temperatures exceed a warming of 2 to 3&#160;°C above pre-industrial temperature levels.<sup id="cite_ref-fischlin_executive_summary_145-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fischlin_executive_summary-145">[145]</a></sup> The uncertainties in this estimate, however, are large: for a rise of about 2&#160;°C the percentage may be as low as 10%, or for about 3&#160;°C, as high as 40%, and depending on <a href="/wiki/Biota_(ecology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Biota (ecology)">biota</a> (all living <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a> of an area, the <a href="/wiki/Flora" title="Flora">flora</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fauna" title="Fauna">fauna</a> considered as a unit)<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> the range is between 1% and 80%.<sup id="cite_ref-global_ecosystem_impacts_148-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-global_ecosystem_impacts-148">[148]</a></sup> As global average temperature exceeds <a href="/wiki/4_Degrees_and_Beyond_International_Climate_Conference" title="4 Degrees and Beyond International Climate Conference">4&#160;°C</a> above pre-industrial levels, model projections suggested that there could be significant extinctions (40-70% of species that were assessed) around the globe.<sup id="cite_ref-global_ecosystem_impacts_148-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-global_ecosystem_impacts-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<p>Assessing whether future changes in ecosystems will be beneficial or detrimental is largely based on how ecosystems are valued by human society.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> For increases in global average temperature exceeding 1.5 to 2.5&#160;°C (relative to global temperatures over the years 1980-1999)<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> and in concomitant atmospheric <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> concentrations, projected changes in ecosystems will have predominantly negative consequences for biodiversity and ecosystems goods and <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">services</a>, e.g., water and food supply.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Abrupt_or_irreversible_changes">Abrupt or irreversible changes</span></h2>
<p>Physical, ecological and social systems may respond in an abrupt, <a href="/wiki/Non-linear" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-linear">non-linear</a> or irregular way to climate change.<sup id="cite_ref-singularities_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-singularities-153">[153]</a></sup> This is as opposed to a smooth or regular response. A quantitative entity behaves "irregularly" when its <a href="/wiki/System_dynamics" title="System dynamics">dynamics</a> are <a href="/wiki/Continuous_function" title="Continuous function">discontinuous</a> (i.e., not smooth), nondifferentiable, unbounded, wildly varying, or otherwise ill-defined.<sup id="cite_ref-singularities_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-singularities-153">[153]</a></sup> Such behaviour is often termed "<a href="/wiki/Mathematical_singularity" class="mw-redirect" title="Mathematical singularity">singular</a>". Irregular behaviour in Earth systems may give rise to certain thresholds, which, when crossed, may lead to a large change in the system.</p>
<p>Some singularities could potentially lead to severe impacts at regional or global scales.<sup id="cite_ref-white_et_al_singularities_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-white_et_al_singularities-154">[154]</a></sup> Examples of "large-scale" singularities are discussed in the articles on <a href="/wiki/Abrupt_climate_change" title="Abrupt climate change">abrupt climate change</a>, <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_feedback" title="Climate change feedback">climate change feedback</a> and <a href="/wiki/Runaway_climate_change" title="Runaway climate change">runaway climate change</a>. It is possible that human-induced climate change could trigger large-scale singularities, but the probabilities of triggering such events are, for the most part,<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> poorly understood.<sup id="cite_ref-white_et_al_singularities_154-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-white_et_al_singularities-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>With low to medium confidence, Smith <i>et al.</i> (2001)<sup id="cite_ref-singularities_153-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-singularities-153">[153]</a></sup> concluded that a rapid warming of more than 3&#160;°C above 1990 levels would exceed thresholds that would lead to large-scale discontinuities in the climate system. Since the assessment by Smith <i>et al.</i> (2001), improved scientific understanding provides more guidance for two large-scale singularities: the role of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a> feedbacks in future climate change (discussed below in the section on biogeochemical cycles) and the melting of the <a href="/wiki/Greenland_ice_sheet" title="Greenland ice sheet">Greenland</a> and <a href="/wiki/West_Antarctic_ice_sheet" class="mw-redirect" title="West Antarctic ice sheet">West Antarctic ice sheets</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-update_of_reasons_for_concern_141-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-update_of_reasons_for_concern-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biogeochemical_cycles">Biogeochemical cycles</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_feedback" title="Climate change feedback">climate change feedback</a></div>
<p>Climate change may have an effect on the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a> in an interactive "feedback" process. A feedback exists where an initial process triggers changes in a second process that in turn influences the initial process. A positive feedback intensifies the original process, and a negative feedback reduces it.<sup id="cite_ref-IPCC_AR4_Synthesis_Report_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_AR4_Synthesis_Report-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:78</sup> Models suggest that the interaction of the climate system and the carbon cycle is one where the feedback effect is positive.<sup id="cite_ref-Assessing_key_vulnerabilities_and_the_risk_from_climate_change_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Assessing_key_vulnerabilities_and_the_risk_from_climate_change-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:792</sup></p>
<p>Using the A2 SRES emissions scenario, Schneider <i>et al.</i> (2007)<sup id="cite_ref-Assessing_key_vulnerabilities_and_the_risk_from_climate_change_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Assessing_key_vulnerabilities_and_the_risk_from_climate_change-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:789</sup> found that this effect led to additional warming by the years 2090-2100 (relative to the 1990–2000) of 0.1–1.5&#160;°C. This estimate was made with high confidence. The climate projections made in the IPCC Fourth Assessment Report summarized earlier of 1.1–6.4&#160;°C account for this feedback effect. On the other hand, with medium confidence, Schneider <i>et al.</i> (2007)<sup id="cite_ref-Assessing_key_vulnerabilities_and_the_risk_from_climate_change_156-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Assessing_key_vulnerabilities_and_the_risk_from_climate_change-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:789</sup> commented that additional releases of GHGs were possible from permafrost, peat lands, wetlands, and large stores of marine hydrates at high latitudes.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greenland_and_West_Antarctic_Ice_sheets">Greenland and West Antarctic Ice sheets</span></h3>
<p>With medium confidence, authors of AR4<sup id="cite_ref-magnitudes_of_impact_49-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-magnitudes_of_impact-49">[49]</a></sup> concluded that with a global average temperature increase of 1–4&#160;°C (relative to temperatures over the years 1990–2000), at least a partial deglaciation of the <a href="/wiki/Greenland_ice_sheet" title="Greenland ice sheet">Greenland ice sheet</a>, and possibly the <a href="/wiki/West_Antarctic_ice_sheet" class="mw-redirect" title="West Antarctic ice sheet">West Antarctic ice sheets</a> would occur. The estimated timescale for partial deglaciation was centuries to millennia, and would contribute 4 to 6 metres (13 to 20&#160;ft) or more to sea level rise over this period.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atlantic_Meridional_Overturning_Circulation">Atlantic Meridional Overturning Circulation</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Shutdown_of_thermohaline_circulation" title="Shutdown of thermohaline circulation">Shutdown of thermohaline circulation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thermohaline_Circulation_2.png" class="image"><img alt="refer to caption" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Thermohaline_Circulation_2.png/320px-Thermohaline_Circulation_2.png" width="320" height="201" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Thermohaline_Circulation_2.png/480px-Thermohaline_Circulation_2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Thermohaline_Circulation_2.png/640px-Thermohaline_Circulation_2.png 2x" data-file-width="1100" data-file-height="690" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thermohaline_Circulation_2.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
This map shows the general location and direction of the warm surface (red) and cold deep water (blue) currents of the <a href="/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation" title="Thermohaline circulation">thermohaline circulation</a>. <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">Salinity</a> is represented by color in units of the Practical Salinity Scale. Low values (blue) are less saline, while high values (orange) are more saline.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Atlantic Meridional Overturning Circulation (AMOC) is an important component of the Earth's climate system, characterized by a northward flow of warm, salty water in the upper layers of the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_ocean" class="mw-redirect" title="Atlantic ocean">Atlantic</a> and a southward flow of colder water in the deep Atlantic.<sup id="cite_ref-ccsp_abrupt_climate_change_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ccsp_abrupt_climate_change-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:5</sup> The AMOC is equivalently known as the <a href="/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation" title="Thermohaline circulation">thermohaline circulation</a> (THC).<sup id="cite_ref-update_of_reasons_for_concern_141-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-update_of_reasons_for_concern-141">[141]</a></sup> Potential impacts associated with MOC changes include reduced warming or (in the case of abrupt change) absolute cooling of northern high-latitude areas near <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> and north-western Europe, an increased warming of <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a> high-latitudes, tropical drying, as well as changes to <a href="/wiki/Marine_ecosystem" title="Marine ecosystem">marine ecosystems</a>, terrestrial vegetation, oceanic <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> uptake, oceanic oxygen concentrations, and shifts in fisheries.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> According to an assessment by the US <a href="/wiki/Climate_Change_Science_Program" title="Climate Change Science Program">Climate Change Science Program</a> (CCSP, 2008b),<sup id="cite_ref-ccsp_abrupt_climate_change_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ccsp_abrupt_climate_change-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:5</sup> it is very likely (greater than 90% probability, based on expert judgement)<sup id="cite_ref-ccsp_abrupt_climate_change_158-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ccsp_abrupt_climate_change-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:12</sup> that the strength of the AMOC will decrease over the course of the 21st century. Warming is still expected to occur over most of the European region downstream of the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Current" title="North Atlantic Current">North Atlantic Current</a> in response to increasing GHGs, as well as over <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>. Although it is very unlikely (less than 10% probability, based on expert judgement)<sup id="cite_ref-ccsp_abrupt_climate_change_158-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ccsp_abrupt_climate_change-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:12</sup> that the AMOC will collapse in the 21st century, the potential consequences of such a collapse could be severe.<sup id="cite_ref-ccsp_abrupt_climate_change_158-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ccsp_abrupt_climate_change-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:5</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Irreversibilities">Irreversibilities</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Commitment_to_radiative_forcing">Commitment to radiative forcing</span></h4>
<p>Emissions of GHGs are a potentially irreversible commitment to sustained <a href="/wiki/Radiative_forcing" title="Radiative forcing">radiative forcing</a> in the future.<sup id="cite_ref-albritton_irreversible_radiative_forcing_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-albritton_irreversible_radiative_forcing-160">[160]</a></sup> The contribution of a GHG to radiative forcing depends on the gas's ability to trap <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> (heat) radiation, the concentration of the gas in the atmosphere, and the length of time the gas resides in the atmosphere.<sup id="cite_ref-albritton_irreversible_radiative_forcing_160-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-albritton_irreversible_radiative_forcing-160">[160]</a></sup></p>
<p><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> is the most important anthropogenic GHG.<sup id="cite_ref-ipcc_2007_causes_of_climate_change_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc_2007_causes_of_climate_change-161">[161]</a></sup> While more than half of the <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> emitted is currently removed from the atmosphere within a century, some fraction (about 20%) of emitted <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> remains in the atmosphere for many thousands of years.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> Consequently, <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> emitted today is potentially an irreversible commitment to sustained radiative forcing over thousands of years.</p>
<p>This commitment may not be truly irreversible should techniques be developed to remove <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> or other GHGs directly from the atmosphere, or to block sunlight to induce cooling.<sup id="cite_ref-solomon_irreversible_climate_change_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-solomon_irreversible_climate_change-32">[32]</a></sup> Techniques of this sort are referred to as <a href="/wiki/Climate_engineering" title="Climate engineering">geoengineering</a>. Little is known about the effectiveness, costs or potential <a href="/wiki/Unintended_consequences" title="Unintended consequences">side-effects</a> of geoengineering options.<sup id="cite_ref-barker_geoengineering_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-barker_geoengineering-163">[163]</a></sup> Some geoengineering options, such as blocking sunlight, would not prevent further <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">ocean acidification</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-barker_geoengineering_163-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-barker_geoengineering-163">[163]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Irreversible_impacts">Irreversible impacts</span></h4>
<p>Human-induced climate change may lead to irreversible impacts on physical, biological, and social systems.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> There are a number of examples of climate change impacts that may be irreversible, at least over the timescale of many human generations.<sup id="cite_ref-schneider_irreversibilities_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schneider_irreversibilities-165">[165]</a></sup> These include the large-scale singularities described above – changes in carbon cycle feedbacks, the melting of the Greenland and West Antarctic ice sheets, and changes to the AMOC.<sup id="cite_ref-schneider_irreversibilities_165-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schneider_irreversibilities-165">[165]</a></sup> In biological systems, the extinction of species would be an irreversible impact.<sup id="cite_ref-schneider_irreversibilities_165-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schneider_irreversibilities-165">[165]</a></sup> In social systems, unique <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">cultures</a> may be lost due to climate change.<sup id="cite_ref-schneider_irreversibilities_165-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schneider_irreversibilities-165">[165]</a></sup> For example, humans living on <a href="/wiki/Atoll" title="Atoll">atoll</a> islands face risks due to sea-level rise, sea-surface warming, and increased frequency and intensity of extreme weather events.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Benefits_of_global_warming">Benefits of global warming<span id="Benefits_of_Global_Warming"></span></span></h2>
<p>In some locations and industries global warming may increase productivity, though the IPCC cautions that "Estimates agree on the size of the impact (small relative to economic growth), and 17 of the 20 impact estimates shown in Figure 10-1 are negative. Losses accelerate with greater warming, and estimates diverge."<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> The identified benefits are listed below.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="CO2_fertilisation_effect">CO2 fertilisation effect</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">CO2</a> is one of the substances which plants require to grow. Increasing its amount in the air contributes to:</p>
<ul>
<li>Improved agriculture in some high latitude regions <sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup></li>
<li>Increased growing season in <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> <sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></li>
<li>Increased productivity of <a href="/wiki/Bitter_orange" title="Bitter orange">sour orange</a> trees <sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup></li>
<li>Increased vegetation activity in high northern latitudes <sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup></li>
<li>Increased plankton biomass in the North Pacific Subtropical Gyre <sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup></li>
<li>Recent increase in forest growth <sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup></li>
<li>Increased Arctic tundra plant reproduction <sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_health">Human health</span></h3>
<ul>
<li>Winter deaths might decline as temperatures warm. However, this is disputed for at least some regions. For example <sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> states "Although excess winter deaths evidently do exist, winter cold severity no longer predicts the numbers affected. We conclude that no evidence exists that excess winter deaths in England and Wales will fall if winters warm with climate change."</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ice-free_Northwest_Passage">Ice-free Northwest Passage</span></h3>
<ul>
<li>Ships will travel on a shorter route between the Pacific and Atlantic oceans <sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animal_population_changes">Animal population changes</span></h3>
<p>Some animals will benefit from the warming:</p>
<ul>
<li>Increase in chinstrap and gentoo penguins <sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup></li>
<li>Bigger marmots <sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_opinion">Scientific opinion</span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a> (IPCC) has published several major assessments on the effects of global warming.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> Its most recent comprehensive impact assessment was published in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> Publications describing the effects of climate change have also been produced by the following organizations:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/American_Association_for_the_Advancement_of_Science" title="American Association for the Advancement of Science">American Association for the Advancement of Science</a> (AAAS)<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup></li>
<li>A report by the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands_Environmental_Assessment_Agency" title="Netherlands Environmental Assessment Agency">Netherlands Environmental Assessment Agency</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Netherlands_Meteorological_Institute" title="Royal Netherlands Meteorological Institute">Royal Netherlands Meteorological Institute</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wageningen_University_and_Research_Centre" title="Wageningen University and Research Centre">Wageningen University and Research Centre</a><sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup></li>
<li>UK AVOID research programme<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup></li>
<li>A report by the UK <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> and <a href="/wiki/US_National_Academy_of_Sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="US National Academy of Sciences">US National Academy of Sciences</a><sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/University_of_New_South_Wales" title="University of New South Wales">University of New South Wales</a> Climate Change Research Centre<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/US_National_Research_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="US National Research Council">US National Research Council</a><sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>A report by Molina <i>et al.</i> (no date)<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> states:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>The overwhelming evidence of human-caused climate change documents both current impacts with significant costs and extraordinary future risks to society and natural systems</p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="NASA_data_and_tools">NASA data and tools</span></h3>
<p>NASA has released public data and tools to predict how temperature and rainfall patterns worldwide may change through to the year 2100 caused by increasing carbon dioxide in Earth’s atmosphere. The dataset shows projected changes worldwide on a regional level simulated by 21 climate models. The data can be viewed on a daily timescale for individual cities and towns and may be used to conduct climate risk assessments to predict the local and global effects of weather dangers, for example droughts, floods, heat waves and declines in agriculture productivity, and help plan responses to global warming effects.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Footnotes">Footnotes</span></h2>
<p><sup>1</sup> The TAR<sup id="cite_ref-Uncertainty_in_the_TAR_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uncertainty_in_the_TAR-190">[190]</a></sup> and AR4<sup id="cite_ref-ipcc_ar4_uncertainty_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc_ar4_uncertainty-29">[29]</a></sup> that are referred to in this article use specific and quantitative language to describe uncertainty. This language is intended to provide an indication of the level of confidence that IPCC authors have about a particular finding. The qualitative language used to describe uncertainty has a quantitative scale associated with it. The quantitative values for qualitative terms are intended to ensure that confidence levels are interpreted correctly. The is because qualitative statements, e.g., using the word "likely", can be interpreted differently in quantitative terms.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:44</sup></p>
<p>Confidence levels used in the TAR:<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Very High = 95% or greater</li>
<li>High = 67-95%</li>
<li>Medium = 33-67%</li>
<li>Low = 5-33%</li>
<li>Very Low = 5% or less</li>
</ul>
<p>Confidence statements made in AR4 are listed below:<sup id="cite_ref-ipcc_ar4_uncertainty_29-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc_ar4_uncertainty-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Very high confidence: At least 9 out of 10 chance of being correct</li>
<li>High confidence: About 8 out of 10 chance " " "</li>
<li>Medium confidence: About 5 out of 10 chance " " "</li>
<li>Low confidence: About 2 out of 10 chance " " "</li>
<li>Very low confidence: Less than a 1 out of 10 chance " " "</li>
</ul>
<p>IPCC (2012)<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> uses the following terms: "very low", "low", "medium", "high", and "very high confidence". Unlike the TAR and AR4, the scale is qualitative rather than quantitative.</p>
<p>The quantitative values used by IPCC authors are "subjective" probabilities,<sup id="cite_ref-ipcc_ar4_uncertainty_29-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc_ar4_uncertainty-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Uncertainty_in_the_TAR_190-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uncertainty_in_the_TAR-190">[190]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> also known as "personalist" or "<a href="/wiki/Bayesian_probability" title="Bayesian probability">Bayesian</a>" probabilities,<sup id="cite_ref-US_CCSP_report_on_uncertainty_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-US_CCSP_report_on_uncertainty-195">[195]</a></sup> and reflect the expert judgement of IPCC authors. In this formulation, probability is not only a function of an event, but also the state of information that is available to the person making the assessment.<sup id="cite_ref-US_CCSP_report_on_uncertainty_195-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-US_CCSP_report_on_uncertainty-195">[195]</a></sup> In this framework, assigned probabilities may change as more or different information becomes available.<sup id="cite_ref-US_CCSP_report_on_uncertainty_195-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-US_CCSP_report_on_uncertainty-195">[195]</a></sup></p>
<p>The IPCC also uses another scale to describe the likelihood of a particular event occurring.<sup id="cite_ref-ipcc_ar4_uncertainty_29-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc_ar4_uncertainty-29">[29]</a></sup> This is different from the confidence scales described above, and it is possible to assign confidence values to statements of likelihood. For example, the judgement that an event is improbable (e.g., rolling a <a href="/wiki/Dice" title="Dice">dice</a> twice and getting a six both times) may be assigned a high level of scientific confidence.<sup id="cite_ref-ipcc_ar4_uncertainty_29-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc_ar4_uncertainty-29">[29]</a></sup> Also, the probability that an event has an even chance of occurring (e.g., a tossed <a href="/wiki/Coin" title="Coin">coin</a> coming up heads) may also be assigned a high level of confidence.<sup id="cite_ref-ipcc_ar4_uncertainty_29-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc_ar4_uncertainty-29">[29]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="External_links">External links</span></h2>
<dl>
<dt>Physical impacts</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.wmo.int/pages/themes/climate/main_climate_change.php">"Climate Change"</a>. <a href="/wiki/World_Meteorological_Organization" title="World Meteorological Organization">World Meteorological Organization</a>.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.ipcc-wg1.unibe.ch/">The IPCC Working Group I (WG I)</a>. This body assesses the physical scientific aspects of the climate system and climate change.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://nex.nasa.gov/nex/">NASA Nex Climate Data and Prediction Models</a></li>
<li>US Navy Climate Change Roadmap, 2010, <a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="http://www.navy.mil/navydata/documents/CCR.pdf">[3]</a></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dt>Social, economic and ecological impacts</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.un.org/esa/dsd/dsd_aofw_cc/cc_index.shtml">Climate change</a> on the United Nations Economic and Social Development (UNESD) Division for Sustainable Development website.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.ipcc-wg2.gov/">The IPCC Working Group II (WG II) website</a> – This body assesses the vulnerability of socio-economic and natural systems to climate change, negative and positive consequences of climate change, and options for adapting to it.</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dt>General</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://esa.un.org/subindex/pgViewSites.asp?termCode=QB.25">List of United Nations Functional Commissions and Expert Bodies related to climate change</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/IRIN" title="IRIN">IRIN</a>, the humanitarian news and analysis service of the UN Office for the Coordination of Humanitarian Affairs: <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.irinnews.org/Report.aspx?ReportId=79563">"What climate change does"</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.irinnews.org/Report.aspx?ReportId=79508">"How climate change works"</a>, and <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.irinnews.org/IndepthMain.aspx?IndepthId=73&amp;ReportId=78246">"Gathering Storm - the humanitarian impact of climate change"</a></li>
<li>Videos:
<ul>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://video.google.com/videoplay?docid=4119472365452589212">"Educational Forum: Arctic Climate Impact"</a>. Panel discussion with James J. McCarthy, Professor at Harvard University, and Author; Paul R. Epstein, M.D., instructor in medicine at Harvard Medical School; and Ross Gelbspan, Pulitzer Prize–winning journalist and author. Massachusetts School of Law.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://climateprogress.org/2010/02/05/videos-humans-are-changing-the-climate-global-warming-threat-chris-field/#more-18551">"How we know humans are changing the climate and Why climate change is a clear and present danger"</a>. Interviews with Christopher Field and Michael MacCracken. Christopher Field is the director of the Department of Global Ecology at the Carnegie Institution of Washington, professor of biology and environmental earth system science at Stanford University, and the Working Group II Co-Chair for the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change. Michael MacCracken is the chief scientist for Climate Change Programs at the Climate Institute and a co-author and contributing author for various chapters in the IPCC assessment reports. Climate Progress website, February 5, 2010.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.businessinsider.com/terrible-effects-of-climate-change-2014-10">25 Devastating Effects Of Climate Change</a>—<i><a href="/wiki/Business_Insider" title="Business Insider">Business Insider</a></i> (October 11, 2014)</li>
</ul>
